        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">
          Neo-Aramaic
         </a>
         <br/>
         <span style="font-size:90%;">
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">
           Assyrian
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">

       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#b0c4de;">
         Religion
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         Mainly
         <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
          Christianity
         </a>
         <br/>
         <small>
          (majority:
          <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
           Syriac Christianity
          </a>
          ; minority:
          <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">
           Protestantism
          </a>
          )
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Assyrian people
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
        Syriac
       </a>
       :
       <span dir="rtl" lang="syr">
        ܐܫܘܪܝܐ
       </span>
       ‎), or
       <b>
        Syriacs
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Terms_for_Syriac_Christians" title="Terms for Syriac Christians">
        terms for Syriac Christians
       </a>
       ), are an
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">
        ethnic group
       </a>
       indigenous to the
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minahan_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Minahan-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of them self-identify as
       <b>
        <a href="/wiki/Terms_for_Syriac_Christians#Aramean_identity" title="Terms for Syriac Christians">
         Arameans
        </a>
       </b>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or as
       <b>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Christians" title="Chaldean Christians">
         Chaldeans
        </a>
       </b>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They speak
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Aramaic_language" title="East Aramaic language">
        East Aramaic languages
       </a>
       as well as the dominant languages in their countries of residence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Assyrians are typically
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
        Syriac Christians
       </a>
       who
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_continuity" title="Assyrian continuity">
        claim descent from Assyria
       </a>
       , one of the oldest civilizations in the world, dating back to 2500 BC in ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The areas that form the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">
        Assyrian homeland
       </a>
       are parts of present-day northern
       <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
        Iraq
       </a>
       , southeastern
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       , northwestern
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       and, more recently, northeastern
       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
        Syria
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.ca_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.ca-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skutsch2013_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Skutsch2013-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The majority have migrated to other regions of the world, including
       <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
        North America
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
        Levant
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">
        Caucasus
       </a>
       during the past century.
       <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">
        Emigration
       </a>
       was triggered by events such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Diyarbak%C4%B1r_(1895)" title="Massacres of Diyarbakır (1895)">
        Massacres of Diyarbakır
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">
        Assyrian Genocide
       </a>
       (concurrent with the
       <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">
        Armenian
       </a>
       &amp;
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">
        Greek Genocide
       </a>
       ) during
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
        World War I
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       and allied Kurdish tribes, the
       <a href="/wiki/Simele_massacre" title="Simele massacre">
        Simele Massacre
       </a>
       in Iraq in 1933, the
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">
        Iranian Revolution
       </a>
       of 1979, Arab Nationalist
       <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athism" title="Ba'athism">
        Ba'athist
       </a>
       policies in Iraq and Syria, the rise of
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">
        Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant
       </a>
       (ISIS) and its takeover of most of the
       <a href="/wiki/Nineveh_plains" title="Nineveh plains">
        Nineveh plains
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Assyrians are predominantly Christian, mostly adhering to the
       <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
        East
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/West_Syrian_Rite" title="West Syrian Rite">
        West Syrian
       </a>
       liturgical rites of
       <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
        Christianity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The churches that constitute the East Syrian rite include the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
        Assyrian Church of the East
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">
        Ancient Church of the East
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
        Chaldean Catholic Church
       </a>
       , whereas the churches of the West Syrian rite are the
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">
        Syriac Orthodox Church
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">
        Syriac Catholic Church
       </a>
       . Both rites use
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
        Classical Syriac
       </a>
       as their liturgical language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most recently, the 2003
       <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">
        Iraq War
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">
        Syrian Civil War
       </a>
       , which began in 2011, have displaced much of the remaining Assyrian community from their homeland as a result of ethnic and religious persecution at the hands of
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_extremism" title="Islamic extremism">
        Islamic extremists
       </a>
       . Of the one million or more Iraqis reported by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
        United Nations
       </a>
       to have fled Iraq since the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Iraq_(2003%E2%80%9311)" title="History of Iraq (2003–11)">
        occupation
       </a>
       , nearly 40% were Assyrians even though Assyrians comprised only around 3% of the pre-war Iraqi demography.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Assyrian_Report_on_CWN_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Assyrian_Report_on_CWN-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to a 2013 report by a
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Syriac_Assyrian_Popular_Council" title="Chaldean Syriac Assyrian Popular Council">
        Chaldean Syriac Assyrian Popular Council
       </a>
       official, it is estimated that only 300,000 Assyrians remain in Iraq.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ishtartv.com_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ishtartv.com-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of the emergence of ISIS and the taking over of much of the Assyrian homeland by the terror group, another major wave of Assyrian displacement has taken place. ISIS was driven out from the Assyrian villages in the
       <a href="/wiki/Khabur_(Euphrates)" title="Khabur (Euphrates)">
        Khabour River
       </a>
       Valley and the areas surrounding the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Hasakah" title="Al-Hasakah">
        Al-Hasakah
       </a>
       in Syria by 2015, and from the Nineveh Plains in Iraq by 2017. Since the expulsion of ISIS, the Nineveh Plains have been divided into Iraqi and Kurdish-controlled zones, with Assyrian militias on both sides. In Gozarto/Northern Syria, Assyrian groups have been taking part both politically and militarily in the Kurdish-dominated but multiethnic
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Federation_of_Northern_Syria" title="Democratic Federation of Northern Syria">
        Democratic Federation of Northern Syria
       </a>
       project.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Assyrian_people" title="History of the Assyrian people">
        History of the Assyrian people
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Christian_history">
        Pre-Christian history
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
        Achaemenid Empire
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
        Achaemenid Assyria
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">
        Neo-Assyrian Empire
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sculpted_reliefs_depicting_Ashurbanipal,_the_last_great_Assyrian_king,_hunting_lions,_gypsum_hall_relief_from_the_North_Palace_of_Nineveh_(Irak),_c._645-635_BC,_British_Museum_(16722368932).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3178" data-file-width="4811" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sculpted_reliefs_depicting_Ashurbanipal%2C_the_last_great_Assyrian_king%2C_hunting_lions%2C_gypsum_hall_relief_from_the_North_Palace_of_Nineveh_%28Irak%29%2C_c._645-635_BC%2C_British_Museum_%2816722368932%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sculpted_reliefs_depicting_Ashurbanipal%2C_the_last_great_Assyrian_king%2C_hunting_lions%2C_gypsum_hall_relief_from_the_North_Palace_of_Nineveh_%28Irak%29%2C_c._645-635_BC%2C_British_Museum_%2816722368932%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Sculpted_reliefs_depicting_Ashurbanipal%2C_the_last_great_Assyrian_king%2C_hunting_lions%2C_gypsum_hall_relief_from_the_North_Palace_of_Nineveh_%28Irak%29%2C_c._645-635_BC%2C_British_Museum_%2816722368932%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sculpted_reliefs_depicting_Ashurbanipal,_the_last_great_Assyrian_king,_hunting_lions,_gypsum_hall_relief_from_the_North_Palace_of_Nineveh_(Irak),_c._645-635_BC,_British_Museum_(16722368932).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Part of the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Lion_Hunt_of_Ashurbanipal" title="Lion Hunt of Ashurbanipal">
           Lion Hunt of Ashurbanipal
          </a>
         </i>
         , c. 645-635 BC
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In prehistoric times, the region that was to become known as
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">
        Assyria
       </a>
       (and Subartu) was home to
       <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">
        Neanderthals
       </a>
       such as the remains of those which have been found at the
       <a href="/wiki/Shanidar_Cave" title="Shanidar Cave">
        Shanidar Cave
       </a>
       . The earliest
       <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">
        Neolithic
       </a>
       sites in Assyria belonged to the
       <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">
        Jarmo
       </a>
       culture c. 7100 BC and
       <a href="/wiki/Tell_Hassuna" title="Tell Hassuna">
        Tell Hassuna
       </a>
       , the centre of the
       <a href="/wiki/Hassuna_culture" title="Hassuna culture">
        Hassuna culture
       </a>
       , c. 6000 BC.
      </p>
      <p>
       The history of Assyria begins with the formation of the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
        Assur
       </a>
       perhaps as early as the 25th century BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_kings" title="List of Assyrian kings">
        Assyrian king list
       </a>
       records kings dating from the 25th century BC onwards, the earliest being
       <a href="/wiki/Tudiya" title="Tudiya">
        Tudiya
       </a>
       , who was a contemporary of
       <a href="/wiki/Ibrium" title="Ibrium">
        Ibrium
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">
        Ebla
       </a>
       . However, many of these early kings would have been local rulers, and from the late 24th century BC to the early 22nd century BC, they were usually subjects of the
       <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">
        Akkadian Empire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       During the early
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">
        Bronze Age
       </a>
       period,
       <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">
        Sargon of Akkad
       </a>
       united all the native
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic_language" title="Semitic language">
        Semitic
       </a>
       -speaking peoples and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">
        Sumerians
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       (including the Assyrians) under the
       <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">
        Akkadian Empire
       </a>
       (2335-2154 BC). The cities of Assur and
       <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
        Nineveh
       </a>
       , together with a number of other towns and cities, existed as early as the 25th century BC, although they appear to have been Sumerian-ruled administrative centres at this time, rather than independent states. The Sumerians were eventually absorbed into the Akkadian (Assyro-Babylonian) population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From 1700 BC and onward, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
        Sumerian language
       </a>
       was preserved by ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
        Babylonians
       </a>
       and Assyrians only as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" title="Liturgical language">
        liturgical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">
        classical language
       </a>
       for religious, artistic and scholarly purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-woods-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the traditions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
        Assyrian Church of the East
       </a>
       , they are descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">
        Abraham
       </a>
       's grandson (
       <a href="/wiki/Dedanites" title="Dedanites">
        Dedan
       </a>
       son of
       <a href="/wiki/Jokshan" title="Jokshan">
        Jokshan
       </a>
       ), progenitor of the ancient Assyrians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, there is no historical basis for the biblical assertion whatsoever; there is no mention in Assyrian records (which date as far back as the 24th century BC). The Assyrian people, after the fall of their empire, fell under foreign domination ever since. The
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">
        Persian Empire
       </a>
       was founded, which consumed the entire
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">
        Neo-Babylonian or "Chaldean" Empire
       </a>
       in 539 BC. Assyrians became
       <a href="/wiki/Front_line" title="Front line">
        front line
       </a>
       soldiers for the Persian Empire under
       <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">
        Xerxes I
       </a>
       , playing a major role in the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marathon" title="Battle of Marathon">
        Battle of Marathon
       </a>
       under
       <a href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">
        Darius I
       </a>
       in 490 BC.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Assyrian army accounted for three legions of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">
        Roman army
       </a>
       , defending the
       <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">
        Parthian
       </a>
       border. In the 1st century, it was the Assyrian army that enabled
       <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">
        Vespasian
       </a>
       's coup. From the later 2nd century, the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">
        Roman Senate
       </a>
       included several notable Assyrians, including
       <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Pompeianus" title="Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus">
        Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">
        Avidius Cassius
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 1st century BC, Assyria was the theatre of the protracted
       <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">
        Roman–Persian Wars
       </a>
       . It would become
       <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">
        a Roman province
       </a>
       (Assyria Provincia) from 116 to 363 AD. Despite the influx of foreign elements, the presence of Assyrians is confirmed by the worship of the god
       <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(god)" title="Ashur (god)">
        Ashur
       </a>
       , all proof of the continuity of the Assyrians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Greeks, Parthians, and Romans had a rather low-level of integration with the local population in Mesopotamia, which allowed their cultures to survive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christian_period">
        Early Christian period
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1041" data-file-width="1788" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg/230px-Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg/345px-Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg/460px-Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Southwestern_part_of_the_Sasanian_Empire.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of
         <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" title="Asōristān">
          Asōristān
         </a>
         (226 AD-637 AD).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
        Syriac Christianity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Eastern_Christianity" title="History of Eastern Christianity">
        History of Eastern Christianity
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" title="Asōristān">
        Asōristān
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Assyrians were Christianized in the first to third centuries in
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">
        Roman Syria
       </a>
       and Roman Assyria. The population of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">
        Sasanian province
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/As%C5%8Drist%C4%81n" title="Asōristān">
        Asōristān
       </a>
       was a mixed one, composed of Assyrians,
       <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
        Arameans
       </a>
       in the far south and the western deserts, and
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
        Persians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RP_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RP-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
        Greek
       </a>
       element in the cities, still strong during the
       <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">
        Parthian Empire
       </a>
       , ceased to be ethnically distinct in Sasanian times. The majority of the population were
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic_languages" title="Eastern Aramaic languages">
        Eastern Aramaic speakers
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Along with the Arameans,
       <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">
        Armenians
       </a>
       , Greeks, and
       <a href="/wiki/Nabataeans" title="Nabataeans">
        Nabataeans
       </a>
       , the Assyrians were among the first people to convert to
       <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
        Christianity
       </a>
       and spread
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">
        Eastern Christianity
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">
        Far East
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Seleucia" title="Council of Seleucia">
        Council of Seleucia
       </a>
       of ca. 325 dealt with jurisdictional conflicts among the leading bishops. They were divided by the
       <a href="/wiki/Nestorian_Schism" title="Nestorian Schism">
        Nestorian Schism
       </a>
       in the 5th century, and from the 8th century, they became a
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_religion" title="Minority religion">
        minority religion
       </a>
       following the
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">
        Muslim conquest of Persia
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       At the subsequent
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Seleucia-Ctesiphon" title="Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon">
        Council of Seleucia-Ctesiphon
       </a>
       of 410, the Christian communities of
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       renounced all subjection to Antioch and the "Western" bishops and the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Patriarchs_of_the_Church_of_the_East" title="List of Patriarchs of the Church of the East">
        Bishop of Seleucia-Ctesiphon
       </a>
       (modern
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mada%27in" title="Al-Mada'in">
        al-Mada'in
       </a>
       ) assumed the rank of
       <a href="/wiki/Catholicos" title="Catholicos">
        Catholicos
       </a>
       . Whereas Latin and Greek Christian cultures became protected by the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
        Roman
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine Empires
       </a>
       , respectively, Assyrian Christianity often found itself marginalized and persecuted.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Nestorian schism and
       <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">
        Monophysite
       </a>
       schisms of the 5th century divided the church into separate denominations. With the rise of
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
        Syriac Christianity
       </a>
       , eastern Aramaic enjoyed a renaissance as a classical language in the 2nd to 8th centuries, and the modern Assyrian people continue to speak eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">
        Neo-Aramaic languages
       </a>
       . Assyria continued to exist as a geopolitical entity until the Arab-Islamic conquest in the mid-7th century.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_conquest">
        Arab conquest
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">
        Muslim conquest of Persia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Assyrians initially experienced some periods of religious and cultural freedom interspersed with periods of severe religious and ethnic persecution after the 7th century
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">
        Muslim conquest of Persia
       </a>
       . Assyrians contributed to Islamic civilizations during the
       <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">
        Umayyad
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">
        Abbasid Caliphates
       </a>
       by translating works of
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">
        Greek philosophers
       </a>
       to Syriac and afterwards to
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       . They also excelled in
       <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">
        philosophy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">
        science
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">
        Qusta ibn Luqa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Masawaiyh" title="Masawaiyh">
        Masawaiyh
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoubertRocher1995_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JoubertRocher1995-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Eutychius_of_Alexandria" title="Eutychius of Alexandria">
        Eutychius of Alexandria
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Jabril_ibn_Bukhtishu" title="Jabril ibn Bukhtishu">
        Jabril ibn Bukhtishu
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
        theology
       </a>
       (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tatian" title="Tatian">
        Tatian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bardaisan" title="Bardaisan">
        Bardaisan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Babai_the_Great" title="Babai the Great">
        Babai the Great
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">
        Nestorius
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Marga" title="Thomas of Marga">
        Thomas of Marga
       </a>
       ) and the personal
       <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">
        physicians
       </a>
       of the Abbasid Caliphs were often Assyrians, such as the long-serving
       <a href="/wiki/Bukhtishu" title="Bukhtishu">
        Bukhtishu
       </a>
       dynasty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many scholars of the
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">
        House of Wisdom
       </a>
       were of Assyrian Christian background.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Indigenous Assyrians became second-class citizens (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">
         dhimmi
        </a>
       </i>
       ) in a greater Arab Islamic state, and those who resisted Arabisation and conversion to Islam were subject to severe religious, ethnic and cultural discrimination, and had certain restrictions imposed upon them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Assyrians were excluded from specific duties and occupations reserved for Muslims, they did not enjoy the same political rights as Muslims, their word was not equal to that of a Muslim in legal and civil matters, as Christians they were subject to payment of a special tax (
       <a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">
        jizya
       </a>
       ), they were banned from spreading their religion further or building new churches in Muslim-ruled lands, but were also expected to adhere to the same laws of property, contract and obligation as the Muslim Arabs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They couldn't seek conversion of a Muslim, a non-Muslim man couldn't marry a Muslim woman and the child of such a marriage would be considered Muslim. They couldn't own a Muslim slave and had to wear different clothing from Muslims in order to be distinguishable. In addition to the jizya tax, they were also required to pay the
       <a href="/wiki/Kharaj" title="Kharaj">
        kharaj
       </a>
       tax on their land which was heavier than the jizya. However they were ensured protection, given religious freedom and to govern themselves in accordance to their own laws.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseph_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Joseph-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As non-Islamic
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proselytising" title="Proselytising">
        proselytising
       </a>
       was punishable by death under
       <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">
        Sharia
       </a>
       , the Assyrians were forced into preaching in
       <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">
        Transoxiana
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">
        Central Asia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">
        Mongolia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       where they established numerous churches. The
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">
        Church of the East
       </a>
       was considered to be one of the major Christian powerhouses in the world, alongside Latin Christianity in Europe and the
       <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
        Byzantine Empire
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 7th century AD onwards Mesopotamia saw a steady influx of Arabs,
       <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
        Kurds
       </a>
       and other
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
        Iranian peoples
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and later
       <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">
        Turkic peoples
       </a>
       . Assyrians were increasingly marginalized, persecuted, and gradually became a minority in their own homeland. Conversion to Islam as a result of heavy taxation which also resulted in decreased revenue from their rulers. As a result, the new converts migrated to Muslim garrison towns nearby.
      </p>
      <p>
       Assyrians remained dominant in Upper Mesopotamia as late as the 14th century
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
        Ashur
       </a>
       was still occupied by Assyrians during the Islamic period until the mid-14th century when the
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
        Muslim
       </a>
       Turco-Mongol ruler
       <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">
        Timur
       </a>
       conducted a religiously motivated massacre against Assyrians. After, there were no records of Assyrians remaining in Ashur according to the archaeological and numismatic record. From this point, the Assyrian population was dramatically reduced in their homeland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       From the 19th century, after the rise of nationalism in the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       , the Ottomans started viewing Assyrians and other Christians in their eastern front as a potential threat. The Kurdish Emirs sought to consolidate their power by attacking Assyrian communities which were already well-established there. Scholars estimate that tens of thousands of Assyrian in the
       <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">
        Hakkari
       </a>
       region were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Badr_Khan" title="Massacres of Badr Khan">
        massacred in 1843
       </a>
       when
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedr_Khan_Beg" title="Bedr Khan Beg">
        Bedr Khan Beg
       </a>
       , the emir of
       <a href="/wiki/Bohtan" title="Bohtan">
        Bohtan
       </a>
       , invaded their region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After a later massacre in 1846, the Ottomans were forced by the western powers into intervening in the region, and the ensuing conflict destroyed the Kurdish emirates and reasserted the Ottoman power in the area. The Assyrians were subject to the
       <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Diyarbak%C4%B1r_(1895)" title="Massacres of Diyarbakır (1895)">
        massacres of Diyarbakır
       </a>
       soon after.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Being culturally, ethnically, and linguistically distinct from their Muslim neighbors in the Middle East—the Arabs,
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
        Persians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
        Kurds
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
        Turks
       </a>
       —the Assyrians have endured much hardship throughout their recent history as a result of religious and ethnic persecution by these groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mongolian_and_Turkic_rule">
        Mongolian and Turkic rule
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">
        Timurid Empire
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">
        Aq Qoyunlu
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Kara_Koyunlu" title="Kara Koyunlu">
        Kara Koyunlu
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       After initially coming under the control of the
       <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">
        Seljuk Empire
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">
        Buyid dynasty
       </a>
       , the region eventually came under the control of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">
        Mongol Empire
       </a>
       after the
       <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">
        fall of Baghdad
       </a>
       in 1258. The Mongol khans were sympathetic with Christians and did not harm them. The most prominent among them was probably
       <a href="/wiki/Isa_Kelemechi" title="Isa Kelemechi">
        Isa Kelemechi
       </a>
       , a diplomat, astrologer, and head of the Christian affairs in
       <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">
        Yuan China
       </a>
       . He spent some time in Persia under the
       <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">
        Ilkhanate
       </a>
       . The 14th century massacres of Timur devastated the Assyrian people. Timur's massacres and pillages of all that was Christian drastically reduced their existence. At the end of the reign of Timur, the Assyrian population had almost been eradicated in many places. Toward the end of the thirteenth century,
       <a href="/wiki/Bar_Hebraeus" title="Bar Hebraeus">
        Bar Hebraeus
       </a>
       , the noted Assyrian scholar and hierarch, found "much quietness" in his diocese in Mesopotamia. Syria’s diocese, he wrote, was "wasted."
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The region was later controlled by the in Iran-based Turkic confederations of the
       <a href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">
        Aq Qoyunlu
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kara_Koyunlu" title="Kara Koyunlu">
        Kara Koyunlu
       </a>
       . Subsequently, all Assyrians, like with the rest of the ethnicities living in the former Aq Qoyunlu territories, fell into
       <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">
        Safavid
       </a>
       hands from 1501 and on.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="From_Iranian_Safavid_to_confirmed_Ottoman_rule">
        From Iranian Safavid to confirmed Ottoman rule
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Badr_Khan" title="Massacres of Badr Khan">
        Massacres of Badr Khan
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Diyarbakir_(1895)" title="Massacres of Diyarbakir (1895)">
        Massacres of Diyarbakir (1895)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_Empire" title="Safavid Empire">
        Safavid Empire
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afsharid_Empire" title="Afsharid Empire">
        Afsharid Empire
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Zand_dynasty" title="Zand dynasty">
        Zand dynasty
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">
        Qajar dynasty
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman-Persian_Wars" title="Ottoman-Persian Wars">
        Ottoman-Persian Wars
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">
        Treaty of Zuhab
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Ottomans secured their control over Mesopotamia and Syria in the first half of the 17th century following the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1623%E2%80%9339)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)">
        Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)
       </a>
       and the resulting
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">
        Treaty of Zuhab
       </a>
       . Non-Muslims were organised into
       <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">
        millets
       </a>
       . Syriac Christians, however, were often considered one millet alongside Armenians until the 19th century, when Nestorian, Syriac Orthodox and Chaldeans gained that right as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kennith255_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kennith255-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A religious schism amongs the Assyrians took place in the mid to late 16th century. Dissent over the hereditary succession within the Assyrian Church of the East grew until 1552, when a group of Assyrian bishops, from the northern regions of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amid" title="Amid">
        Amid
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Salmas" title="Salmas">
        Salmas
       </a>
       , elected a priest, Mar
       <a href="/wiki/Shimun_VIII_Yohannan_Sulaqa" title="Shimun VIII Yohannan Sulaqa">
        Yohannan Sulaqa
       </a>
       , as a rival patriarch. To look for a bishop of
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">
        metropolitan
       </a>
       rank to consecrate him patriarch, Sulaqa traveled to the
       <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">
        pope
       </a>
       in Rome and entered into communion with the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       . In 1553 he was consecrated bishop and elevated to the rank of patriarch taking the name of Mar Shimun VIII. He was granted the title of "Patriarch of the Chaldeans," and his church was named the Church of
       <i>
        Athura and Mosul
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mar Shimun VIII Yohannan Sulaqa returned to northern
       <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
        Mesopotamia
       </a>
       in the same year and fixed his seat in Amid. Before being put to death by the partisans of the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
        Assyrian Church of the East
       </a>
       patriarch of
       <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">
        Alqosh
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :
        <span>
         57
        </span>
       </sup>
       he ordained five metropolitan bishops thus beginning a new ecclesiastical hierarchy: the patriarchal line known as the
       <i>
        Shimun line
       </i>
       . The area of influence of this patriarchate soon moved from Amid east, fixing the See, after many places, in the isolated Assyrian village of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qochanis" title="Qochanis">
        Qochanis
       </a>
       . Although this new church eventually drifted away from Rome by 1600 AD and reentered communion with the Assyrian Church, the archbishop of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amid" title="Amid">
        Amid
       </a>
       reinstated relations with Rome in 1672 AD, giving birth to the modern
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
        Chaldean Catholic Church
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 1840s many of the Assyrians living in the mountains of
       <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">
        Hakkari
       </a>
       in the south eastern corner of the Ottoman Empire were massacred by the Kurdish emirs of Hakkari and Bohtan.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another major massacre of Assyrians (and Armenians) in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       occurred between 1894 and 1897 AD by Turkish troops and their Kurdish allies during the rule of Sultan
       <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">
        Abdul Hamid II
       </a>
       . The motives for these massacres were an attempt to reassert
       <a href="/wiki/Pan-Islamism" title="Pan-Islamism">
        Pan-Islamism
       </a>
       in the Ottoman Empire, resentment at the comparative wealth of the ancient indigenous Christian communities, and a fear that they would attempt to secede from the tottering Ottoman Empire. Assyrians were massacred in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diyarbakir" title="Diyarbakir">
        Diyarbakir
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasankeyef" title="Hasankeyef">
        Hasankeyef
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sivas" title="Sivas">
        Sivas
       </a>
       and other parts of Anatolia, by Sultan Abdul Hamid II. These attacks caused the death of over thousands of Assyrians and the forced "Ottomanisation" of the inhabitants of 245 villages. The Turkish troops looted the remains of the Assyrian settlements and these were later stolen and occupied by Kurds. Unarmed Assyrian women and children were raped, tortured and murdered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_and_aftermath">
        World War I and aftermath
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="932" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg/220px-Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg/330px-Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg/440px-Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_Assyrian_Flag.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Assyrian flag prior to the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
          first World War
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="540" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/220px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/330px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/440px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The burning of bodies of Assyrian women
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" title="Assyrian Genocide">
        Assyrian Genocide
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_struggle_for_independence" title="Assyrian struggle for independence">
        Assyrian struggle for independence
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Assyrians suffered a number of religiously and ethnically motivated massacres throughout the 17th, 18th and 19th centuries AD,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       culminating in the large scale
       <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">
        Hamidian massacres
       </a>
       of unarmed men, women and children by
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
        Muslim
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
        Turks
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
        Kurds
       </a>
       in the late 19th century at the hands of the
       <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
        Ottoman Empire
       </a>
       and its associated (largely
       <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
        Kurdish
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
        Arab
       </a>
       ) militias, which further greatly reduced numbers, particularly in southeastern Turkey.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most significant recent persecution against the Assyrian population was the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">
        Assyrian genocide
       </a>
       which occurred during the First World War. Between 275,000 and 300,000 Assyrians were estimated to have been slaughtered by the armies of the Ottoman Empire and their Kurdish allies, totalling up to two-thirds of the entire Assyrian population.
      </p>
      <p>
       This led to a large-scale migration of Turkish-based Assyrian people into countries such as Syria,
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       , and Iraq (where they were to suffer further violent assaults at the hands of the Arabs and Kurds), as well as other neighbouring countries in and around the Middle East such as
       <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
        Armenia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">
        Georgia
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In reaction to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" title="Assyrian Genocide">
        Assyrian Genocide
       </a>
       and lured by
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        British
       </a>
       and Russian promises of an independent nation, the Assyrians led by
       <a href="/wiki/Agha_Petros" title="Agha Petros">
        Agha Petros
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Malik_Khoshaba" title="Malik Khoshaba">
        Malik Khoshaba
       </a>
       of the Bit-
       <a href="/wiki/Tyari" title="Tyari">
        Tyari
       </a>
       tribe, fought alongside the Allies against Ottoman forces in an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_war_of_independence" title="Assyrian war of independence">
        Assyrian war of independence
       </a>
       . Despite being heavily outnumbered and outgunned the Assyrians fought successfully, scoring a number of victories over the Turks and Kurds. This situation continued until their Russian allies left the war, and Armenian resistance broke, leaving the Assyrians surrounded, isolated and cut off from lines of supply. The sizable Assyrian presence in south eastern Anatolia which had endured for over four millennia was thus reduced to no more than 15,000 by the end of World War I.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">
        Modern history
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1413" data-file-width="1413" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg/200px-Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg/300px-Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg/400px-Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_refugees_on_wagon.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Assyrian refugees on a wagon moving to a newly constructed village on the
         <a href="/wiki/Khabur_(Euphrates)" title="Khabur (Euphrates)">
          Khabur
         </a>
         river in Syria.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The majority of Assyrians living in what is today modern Turkey were forced to flee to either Syria or Iraq after the Turkish victory during the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_War_of_Independence" title="Turkish War of Independence">
        Turkish War of Independence
       </a>
       . In 1932, Assyrians refused to become part of the newly formed state of
       <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
        Iraq
       </a>
       and instead demanded their recognition as a nation within a nation. The Assyrian leader
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mar_Eshai_Shimun_XXIII" title="Mar Eshai Shimun XXIII">
        Mar Eshai Shimun XXIII
       </a>
       asked the
       <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">
        League of Nations
       </a>
       to recognize the right of the Assyrians to govern the area known as the "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_triangle" title="Assyrian triangle">
        Assyrian triangle
       </a>
       " in northern Iraq.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" title="Assyrian Levies">
        Assyrian Levies
       </a>
       were founded by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        British
       </a>
       in 1928, with ancient Assyrian military rankings such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rab-shakeh" title="Rab-shakeh">
        Rab-shakeh
       </a>
       , Rab-talia and
       <a href="/wiki/Tartan_(Assyrian)" title="Tartan (Assyrian)">
        Tartan
       </a>
       , being revived for the first time in millennia for this force. The Assyrians were prized by the British rulers for their fighting qualities, loyalty, bravery and discipline,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and were used to help the British put down insurrections among the Arabs and Kurds. During
       <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
        World War II
       </a>
       , eleven Assyrian companies saw action in
       <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">
        Palestine
       </a>
       and another four served in
       <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
        Cyprus
       </a>
       . The Parachute Company was attached to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Marine_Commando" title="Royal Marine Commando">
        Royal Marine Commando
       </a>
       and were involved in fighting in
       <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
        Albania
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">
        Greece
       </a>
       . The Assyrian Levies played a major role in subduing the pro-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">
        Nazi
       </a>
       Iraqi forces at the battle of
       <a href="/wiki/Habbaniyah" title="Habbaniyah">
        Habbaniya
       </a>
       in 1941.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, this cooperation with the British was viewed with suspicion by some leaders of the newly formed
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">
        Kingdom of Iraq
       </a>
       . The tension reached its peak shortly after the formal declaration of independence when hundreds of Assyrian civilians were slaughtered during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simele_Massacre" title="Simele Massacre">
        Simele Massacre
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">
        Iraqi Army
       </a>
       in August 1933. The events lead to the expulsion of
       <a href="/wiki/Shimun_XXIII_Eshai" title="Shimun XXIII Eshai">
        Shimun XXIII Eshai
       </a>
       the Catholicos Patriarch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
        Assyrian Church of the East
       </a>
       to the United States where resided until his death in 1975.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peshitta1_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-peshitta1-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="800" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg/220px-SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg/330px-SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg/440px-SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SyriacChurch-Mosul.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Celebration at a Syriac Orthodox monastery in
         <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
          Mosul
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Syria" title="Ottoman Syria">
          Ottoman Syria
         </a>
         , early 20th century.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The period from the 1940s through to 1963 saw a period of respite for the Assyrians. The regime of President
       <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">
        Abd al-Karim Qasim
       </a>
       in particular saw the Assyrians accepted into mainstream society. Many urban Assyrians became successful businessmen, others were well represented in politics and the military, their towns and villages flourished undisturbed, and Assyrians came to excel, and be over represented in sports.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba'ath Party">
        Ba'ath Party
       </a>
       seized power in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/February_1963_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="February 1963 Iraqi coup d'état">
        Iraq
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/1963_Syrian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1963 Syrian coup d'état">
        Syria
       </a>
       in 1963, introducing laws aimed at suppressing the Assyrian national identity via arabization policies. The giving of traditional Assyrian names was banned and Assyrian schools, political parties, churches and literature were repressed. Assyrians were heavily pressured into identifying as
       <i>
        Iraqi/Syrian Christians
       </i>
       . Assyrians were not recognized as an ethnic group by the governments and they fostered divisions among Assyrians along religious lines (e.g. Assyrian Church of the East vs. Chaldean Catholic Church vs Syriac Orthodox Church).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNHCR1_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UNHCR1-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In response to Baathist persecution, the Assyrians of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zowaa" title="Zowaa">
        Zowaa
       </a>
       movement within the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Democratic_Movement" title="Assyrian Democratic Movement">
        Assyrian Democratic Movement
       </a>
       took up armed struggle against the Iraqi government in 1982 under the leadership of
       <a href="/wiki/Yonadam_Kanna" title="Yonadam Kanna">
        Yonadam Kanna
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and then joined up with the Iraqi-Kurdistan Front in the early 1990s. Yonadam Kanna in particular was a target of the
       <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">
        Saddam Hussein
       </a>
       Ba'ath government for many years.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Anfal_genocide" title="Anfal genocide">
        Anfal campaign
       </a>
       of 1986–1989 in Iraq resulted in 2,000 Assyrians being murdered through its gas campaigns. Over 31 towns and villages, 25 Assyrian monasteries and churches were razed to the ground. Some Assyrians were murdered, others were deported to large cities, and their lands and homes then being appropriated by Arabs and Kurds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">
        21st century
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan,_Armenia.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1936" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/160px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/240px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/320px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG 2x" width="160"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan,_Armenia.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Assyrian Genocide Memorial in
         <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">
          Yerevan
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
          Armenia
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_exodus_from_Iraq" title="Assyrian exodus from Iraq">
        Assyrian exodus from Iraq
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/2008_attacks_on_Christians_in_Mosul" title="2008 attacks on Christians in Mosul">
        2008 attacks on Christians in Mosul
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Since the 2003 Iraq War social unrest and chaos have resulted in the unprovoked persecution of Assyrians in Iraq, mostly by
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">
        Islamic extremists
       </a>
       , (both
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">
        Shia
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">
        Sunni
       </a>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_nationalism" title="Kurdish nationalism">
        Kurdish nationalists
       </a>
       (ex.
       <a href="/wiki/2011_Dohuk_riots" title="2011 Dohuk riots">
        Dohuk Riots of 2011
       </a>
       aimed at Assyrians &amp;
       <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">
        Yazidis
       </a>
       ). In places such as
       <a href="/wiki/Dora,_Baghdad" title="Dora, Baghdad">
        Dora
       </a>
       , a neighborhood in southwestern
       <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">
        Baghdad
       </a>
       , the majority of its Assyrian population has either fled abroad or to northern Iraq, or has been murdered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Islamic resentment over the United States' occupation of Iraq, and incidents such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Jyllands-Posten_Muhammad_cartoons_controversy" title="Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy">
        <i>
         Jyllands-Posten
        </i>
        Muhammad cartoons
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI_Islam_controversy" title="Pope Benedict XVI Islam controversy">
        Pope Benedict XVI Islam controversy
       </a>
       , have resulted in Muslims attacking Assyrian communities. Since the start of the Iraq war, at least 46 churches and monasteries have been bombed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In recent years, the Assyrians in northern Iraq and northeast Syria have become the target of extreme unprovoked
       <a href="/wiki/Islamic_terrorism" title="Islamic terrorism">
        Islamic terrorism
       </a>
       . As a result, Assyrians have taken up arms alongside other groups (such as the Kurds, Turcomans and Armenians) in response to unprovoked attacks by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Qaeda" title="Al Qaeda">
        Al Qaeda
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIS" title="ISIS">
        ISIS
       </a>
       /
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIL" title="ISIL">
        ISIL
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nusra_Front" title="Nusra Front">
        Nusra Front
       </a>
       and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist" title="Terrorist">
        terrorist
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Fundamentalist" title="Islamic Fundamentalist">
        Islamic Fundamentalist
       </a>
       groups. In 2014 Islamic terrorists of ISIS attacked Assyrian towns and villages in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Homeland" title="Assyrian Homeland">
        Assyrian Homeland
       </a>
       of northern Iraq, together with cities such as
       <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
        Mosul
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">
        Kirkuk
       </a>
       which have large Assyrian populations. There have been reports of atrocities committed by ISIS terrorists since, including; beheadings, crucifixions, child murders, rape, forced conversions,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_Cleansing" title="Ethnic Cleansing">
        Ethnic Cleansing
       </a>
       , robbery, and extortion in the form of illegal taxes levied upon non Muslims. Assyrians in Iraq have responded by forming armed militias to defend their territories.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dawronoye" title="Dawronoye">
         Dawronoye
        </a>
       </i>
       modernization movement has a growing influence on Assyrian identity in the 21st century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawronoye_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dawronoye-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is particularly influential in Syria, where the
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Union_Party_(Syria)" title="Syriac Union Party (Syria)">
        Syriac Union Party
       </a>
       (SUP) has become a major political actor in the
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Federation_of_Northern_Syria" title="Democratic Federation of Northern Syria">
        Democratic Federation of Northern Syria
       </a>
       . In August 2016, the
       <i>
        Ourhi Centre
       </i>
       in the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Qamishli" title="Qamishli">
        Zalin
       </a>
       was started by the Assyrian community, to educate teachers in order to make Syriac an optional language of instruction in public schools,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which then started with the 2016/17 academic year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-syriaclanguage_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-syriaclanguage-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With that academic year, states the Rojava Education Committee, "three curriculums have replaced the old one, to include teaching in three languages: Kurdish, Arabic and Assyrian."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Associated with the SUP is the
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Military_Council" title="Syriac Military Council">
        Syriac Military Council
       </a>
       , an Assyrian militia operating in Syria, established in January 2013 to protect and stand up for the national rights of Assyrians in Syria as well as working together with the other communities in Syria to change the current government of
       <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">
        Bashar al-Assad
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since 2015 it is a component of the
       <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Democratic_Forces" title="Syrian Democratic Forces">
        Syrian Democratic Forces
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Homeland">
        Homeland
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">
        Assyrian homeland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_tribes" title="List of Assyrian tribes">
        List of Assyrian tribes
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Proposals_for_Assyrian_autonomy_in_Iraq" title="Proposals for Assyrian autonomy in Iraq">
        Proposals for Assyrian autonomy in Iraq
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="3507" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png/250px-Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png/375px-Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png/500px-Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyria_World_War_1_relief_English.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <i>
          Assyrian Triangle
         </i>
         is the area with the greatest concentration of Assyrians in the Assyrian homeland and where they seek
         <a href="/wiki/Proposals_for_Assyrian_autonomy_in_Iraq" title="Proposals for Assyrian autonomy in Iraq">
          autonomy
         </a>
         today.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">
        Assyrian homeland
       </a>
       constitutes northern
       <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
        Iraq
       </a>
       , southeastern
       <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
        Turkey
       </a>
       , northwestern
       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
        Iran
       </a>
       and northeastern
       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
        Syria
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skutsch2013_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Skutsch2013-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This includes the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">
        ancient
       </a>
       cities of Nineveh (
       <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
        Mosul
       </a>
       ), Nuhadra (
       <a href="/wiki/Dohuk" title="Dohuk">
        Dohuk
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Arrapha" title="Arrapha">
        Arrapha
       </a>
       /Beth Garmai (
       <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">
        Kirkuk
       </a>
       ), Amida (
       <a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakır">
        Diyarbakir
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">
        Edessa
       </a>
       /Urhoy (
       <a href="/wiki/Urfa" title="Urfa">
        Urfa
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">
        Harran
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">
        Nisabina
       </a>
       /Zalin (Nusaybin/
       <a href="/wiki/Qamishli" title="Qamishli">
        Qamishli
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">
        Arbela
       </a>
       (Erbil), and also the
       <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">
        Christianized
       </a>
       settlements from the 5th century AD after the
       <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">
        spread of Islam
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">
        Urmia
       </a>
       in Iran,
       <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">
        Hakkari
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuksekova" title="Yuksekova">
        Yuksekova
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%87ukurca" title="Çukurca">
        Çukurca
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semdinli" title="Semdinli">
        Semdinli
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Uludere" title="Uludere">
        Uludere
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">
        Tur Abdin
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Midyat" title="Midyat">
        Midyat
       </a>
       and
