       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kafro" title="Kafro">
        Kafro
       </a>
       ) in Turkey, among others.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Assyrians have existed in what is now Syria since ancient times
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , but more recent settlement in
       <a href="/wiki/Qamishli" title="Qamishli">
        Qamishli
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Hasakah" title="Al-Hasakah">
        Al-Hasakah
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Qahtaniyah,_al-Hasakah_Governorate" title="Al-Qahtaniyah, al-Hasakah Governorate">
        Al-Qahtaniyah
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Darbasiyah" title="Al Darbasiyah">
        Al Darbasiyah
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Malikiyah" title="Al-Malikiyah">
        Al-Malikiyah
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Amuda" title="Amuda">
        Amuda
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tel_Tamer" title="Tel Tamer">
        Tel Tamer
       </a>
       and a few other small towns in
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Hasakah_Governorate" title="Al-Hasakah Governorate">
        Al-Hasakah Governorate
       </a>
       were populated by Assyrians in the early
       <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">
        20th century
       </a>
       , after the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">
        Assyrian genocide
       </a>
       in 1914, when they were displaced from other areas of their homeland by the Ottoman Turks and Kurdish tribes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the 1930s and 1940s, an influx of Assyrians, mainly those from northern Iraq who were targeted during the
       <a href="/wiki/Simele_massacre" title="Simele massacre">
        Simele massacre
       </a>
       , resettled in northeastern Syrian villages, mainly in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tel_Tamer" title="Tel Tamer">
        Tel Tamer
       </a>
       and smaller villages along the Khabour River Valley.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Assyrians in Syria did not have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_citizenship" title="Syrian citizenship">
        Syrian citizenship
       </a>
       and title to their land until late 1940s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sizable Assyrian populations only remain in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Syria" title="Assyrians in Syria">
        Syria
       </a>
       , where an estimated 400,000 Assyrians live,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-al-monitor.com_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-al-monitor.com-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Iraq" title="Assyrians in Iraq">
        in Iraq
       </a>
       , where an estimated 300,000 Assyrians live.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ishtartv.com_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ishtartv.com-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Iran and Turkey, only small populations remain, with only 20,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Iran" title="Assyrians in Iran">
        Assyrians in Iran
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atourpop_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-atourpop-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a small but growing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Turkey" title="Assyrians in Turkey">
        Assyrian population in Turkey
       </a>
       , where 25,000 Assyrians live.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="916" data-file-width="1024" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg/250px-Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg/375px-Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg/500px-Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Assyria_Paris_Peace_Conference_1919.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Assyro-Chaldean Delegation's map of an independent Assyria, presented at the Paris Peace Conference 1919.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In Tur Abdin, a traditional center of Assyrian culture, there are only 2,500 Assyrians left.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Down from 50,000 in the 1960 census, but up from 1,000 in 1992. This sharp decline is due to an intense conflict between
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkey-PKK_War" title="Turkey-PKK War">
        Turkey and the PKK
       </a>
       in the 1980s. However, There are an estimated 25,000 Assyrians in all of Turkey, with most living in Istanbul.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated18_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated18-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Hakkari's Assyrian population was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnically_cleanse" title="Ethnically cleanse">
        ethnically cleansed
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">
        Assyrian Genocide
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">
        First World War
       </a>
       . Those who survived fled to unaffected areas of Assyrian settlement in Syria, Iran, and Iraq. Many went to neighboring countries in and around the
       <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">
        Caucasus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
        Middle East
       </a>
       like Armenia, Georgia, southern Russia, Lebanon, Jordan. Most Assyrians currently reside in
       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
        the West
       </a>
       due to the centuries of persecution by the neighboring Muslims.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_subgroups">
        Assyrian subgroups
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are three main Assyrian subgroups: Eastern, Western, Chaldean. These subdivisions range from fully or semi-overlapping linguistic, historical, cultural, and religious similarities between each grouping.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The Eastern subgroup historically inhabited the northern
        <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">
         Zagros Mountains
        </a>
        , the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahla_valley" title="Nahla valley">
         Nahla
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Sapna_valley" title="Sapna valley">
         Sapna valleys
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Dohuk" title="Dohuk">
         Nuhadra
        </a>
        , and the Nineveh and Urmia plains speaking
        <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">
         Northeastern Neo-Aramaic
        </a>
        languages. They are religiously diverse, adhering to the
        <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
         East Syriac
        </a>
        churches,
        <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">
         Protestantism
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
         Judaism
        </a>
        , or are
        <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">
         irreligious
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The Chaldean subgroup is a subset of the Eastern one, as they are traditionally speakers of
        <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Neo-Aramaic" title="Northeastern Neo-Aramaic">
         Northeastern Neo-Aramaic
        </a>
        and belong to the
        <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
         East Syriac Rite
        </a>
        . While some today consider themselves as having a distinct Chaldean identity or belonging to a distinct Chaldean culture, many
        <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholics" title="Chaldean Catholics">
         Chaldean Catholics
        </a>
        identify as Assyrian or Chaldo-Assyrian.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
         <a href="#cite_note-105">
          [105]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The Western subgroup historically inhabited
        <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">
         Tur Abdin
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">
         Upper Mesopotamia
        </a>
        speaking
        <a href="/wiki/Central_Neo-Aramaic" title="Central Neo-Aramaic">
         Central Neo-Aramaic
        </a>
        languages. Most adhere to the
        <a href="/wiki/West_Syrian_Rite" title="West Syrian Rite">
         West Syriac
        </a>
        churches, but a number are irreligious.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="806" data-file-width="1062" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg/250px-Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg/375px-Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg/500px-Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_genocide_map-pt.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map depicting Assyrian relocation after Seyfo in 1914.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Persecution">
        Persecution
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Due to their Christian faith and ethnicity, the Assyrians have been persecuted since their adoption of Christianity. During the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_I" title="Yazdegerd I">
        Yazdegerd I
       </a>
       , Christians in Persia were viewed with suspicion as potential Roman subversives, resulting in persecutions while at the same time promoting
       <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">
        Nestorian
       </a>
       Christianity as a buffer between the Churches of Rome and Persia. Persecutions and attempts to impose
       <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
        Zoroastrianism
       </a>
       continued during the reign of
       <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_II" title="Yazdegerd II">
        Yazdegerd II
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the eras of Mongol rule under
       <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">
        Genghis Khan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">
        Timur
       </a>
       , there was indiscriminate slaughter of tens of thousands of Assyrians and destruction of the Assyrian population of northwestern Iran and central and northern Iran.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       More recent persecutions since the 19th century include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Badr_Khan" title="Massacres of Badr Khan">
        Massacres of Badr Khan
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Diyarbak%C4%B1r_(1895)" title="Massacres of Diyarbakır (1895)">
        Massacres of Diyarbakır (1895)
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Adana_massacre" title="Adana massacre">
        Adana massacre
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">
        Assyrian genocide
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simele_Massacre" title="Simele Massacre">
        Simele Massacre
       </a>
       , and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Anfal_campaign" title="Al-Anfal campaign">
        al-Anfal campaign
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora">
        Diaspora
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Diaspora" title="Assyrian Diaspora">
        Assyrian Diaspora
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_settlements" title="List of Assyrian settlements">
        List of Assyrian settlements
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_world_population.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Assyrian_world_population.png/250px-Assyrian_world_population.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Assyrian_world_population.png/375px-Assyrian_world_population.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Assyrian_world_population.png/500px-Assyrian_world_population.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_world_population.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Assyrian world population.
         <br/>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #440055; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          more than 500,000
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #aa00d4; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          100,000–500,000
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #dd55ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          50,000–100,000
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #eeaaff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          10,000–50,000
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F9D6FE; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          less than 10,000
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Since the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" title="Assyrian Genocide">
        Assyrian Genocide
       </a>
       , many Assyrians have left the Middle East entirely for a more safe and comfortable life in the countries of the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
        Western world
       </a>
       . As a result of this, the Assyrian population in the Middle East has decreased dramatically. As of today there are more Assyrians in the diaspora than in their homeland. The largest Assyrian diaspora communities are found in
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrians/Syriacs_in_Sweden" title="Assyrians/Syriacs in Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       (100,000),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demographics_of_Sweden_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Demographics_of_Sweden-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians/Syriacs_in_Germany" title="Assyrians/Syriacs in Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       (100,000),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erzdiözese_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Erzdiözese-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Americans" title="Assyrian Americans">
        United States
       </a>
       (80,000),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Data_Access_and_Dissemination_Systems_DADS_2_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Data_Access_and_Dissemination_Systems_DADS_2-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in
       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
        Australia
       </a>
       (46,000).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By ethnic percentage, the largest Assyrian diaspora communities are located in
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6dert%C3%A4lje" title="Södertälje">
        Södertälje
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_County" title="Stockholm County">
        Stockholm County
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_and_Syriacs_in_Sweden" title="Assyrians and Syriacs in Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       , and in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairfield_City" title="Fairfield City">
        Fairfield City
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">
        Sydney
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Australian" title="Assyrian Australian">
        Australia
       </a>
       , where they are the leading ethnic group in the suburbs of
       <a href="/wiki/Fairfield,_New_South_Wales" title="Fairfield, New South Wales">
        Fairfield
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairfield_Heights" title="Fairfield Heights">
        Fairfield Heights
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Greenfield_Park,_New_South_Wales" title="Greenfield Park, New South Wales">
        Greenfield Park
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is also a sizable Assyrian community in
       <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">
        Melbourne
       </a>
       , Australia (
       <a href="/wiki/Broadmeadows,_Victoria" title="Broadmeadows, Victoria">
        Broadmeadows
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meadow_Heights" title="Meadow Heights">
        Meadow Heights
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Craigieburn,_Victoria" title="Craigieburn, Victoria">
        Craigieburn
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Americans" title="Assyrian Americans">
        United States
       </a>
       , Assyrians are mostly found in
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
        Chicago
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Niles,_Illinois" title="Niles, Illinois">
        Niles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Skokie,_Illinois" title="Skokie, Illinois">
        Skokie
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">
        Detroit
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Heights,_Michigan" title="Sterling Heights, Michigan">
        Sterling Heights
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Bloomfield_Township" title="West Bloomfield Township">
        West Bloomfield Township
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">
        Phoenix
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Modesto,_California" title="Modesto, California">
        Modesto
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Stanislaus_County,_California" title="Stanislaus County, California">
        Stanislaus County
       </a>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Turlock,_California" title="Turlock, California">
        Turlock
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thrown_to_the_Lions_117-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thrown_to_the_Lions-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Furthermore, small Assyrian communities are found in
       <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">
        San Diego
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacramento" title="Sacramento">
        Sacramento
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fresno" title="Fresno">
        Fresno
       </a>
       in the United States,
       <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">
        Toronto
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Canada" title="Assyrians in Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       and also in
       <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
        London
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Assyrians in the United Kingdom">
        UK
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Ealing" title="London Borough of Ealing">
        London Borough of Ealing
       </a>
       ). In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians/Syriacs_in_Germany" title="Assyrians/Syriacs in Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       , pocket-sized Assyrian communities are scattered throughout
       <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">
        Munich
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">
        Frankfurt
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">
        Stuttgart
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">
        Berlin
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wiesbaden" title="Wiesbaden">
        Wiesbaden
       </a>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">
        Paris
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_France" title="Assyrians in France">
        France
       </a>
       , the commune of
       <a href="/wiki/Sarcelles" title="Sarcelles">
        Sarcelles
       </a>
       has a small number of Assyrians.
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_the_Netherlands" title="Assyrians in the Netherlands">
        Assyrians in the Netherlands
       </a>
       mainly live in the east of the country, in the province of
       <a href="/wiki/Overijssel" title="Overijssel">
        Overijssel
       </a>
       . In
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Russia" title="Assyrians in Russia">
        Russia
       </a>
       , small groups of Assyrians mostly reside in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krasnodar_Kray" title="Krasnodar Kray">
        Krasnodar Kray
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">
        Moscow
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brief_History_of_Assyrians_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brief_History_of_Assyrians-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       To note, the Assyrians residing in
       <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
        California
       </a>
       and Russia tend to be from
       <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Assyrians" title="Iranian Assyrians">
        Iran
       </a>
       , whilst those in Chicago and Sydney are predominantly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraqi_Assyrians" title="Iraqi Assyrians">
        Iraqi Assyrians
       </a>
       . The Assyrians in Detroit are primarily
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">
        Chaldean
       </a>
       speakers, who also originate from Iraq. Assyrians in such European countries as Sweden and Germany would usually be Turoyo-speakers or
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Christians_(Middle_East)" title="Syriac Orthodox Christians (Middle East)">
        Western Assyrians
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Identity_and_subdivisions">
        Identity and subdivisions
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">
        Assyrian nationalism
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">
        Arabization
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">
        Turkification
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Kurdification" title="Kurdification">
        Kurdification
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Assyria.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/220px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/330px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/440px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Assyria.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_flag" title="Assyrian flag">
          Assyrian flag
         </a>
         (adopted in 1968).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
          <a href="#cite_note-119">
           [119]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1200" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Syriac-Aramaic_People.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_flag" title="Syriac flag">
          Syriac-Aramean flag
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
          <a href="#cite_note-120">
           [120]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrians of the Middle East and diaspora employ different terms for self-identification based on conflicting beliefs in the origin and identity of their respective communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanDenBerg_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VanDenBerg-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In certain areas of the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_homeland" title="Assyrian homeland">
        Assyrian homeland
       </a>
       , identity within a community depends on a person's village of origin (see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Assyrian_villages" title="List of Assyrian villages">
        List of Assyrian villages
       </a>
       ) or Christian denomination rather than their ethnic commonality, for instance
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic" title="Chaldean Catholic">
        Chaldean Catholics
       </a>
       preferring to be called Chaldeans instead of Assyrians, or a
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Christians_(Middle_East)" title="Syriac Orthodox Christians (Middle East)">
        Syriac Orthodox Christian
       </a>
       preferring to be called a Syriac.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 19th century English archaeologist
       <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">
        Austen Henry Layard
       </a>
       believed that the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Christian" title="Syriac Christian">
        Syriac Christian
       </a>
       communities were descended from the ancient Assyrians, a view that was also shared by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Ainger_Wigram" title="William Ainger Wigram">
        William Ainger Wigram
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross_2005_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cross_2005-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, Assyrians and other minority ethnic groups in the Middle East, feel pressure to identify as "Arabs",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "Turks" and "Kurds".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
        <a href="#cite_note-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Media" title="Western Media">
        Western Media
       </a>
       often makes no mention of any ethnic identity of the Christian people of the region and simply call them Christians,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Monitor_127-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Al-Monitor-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iraqi_Christians" title="Iraqi Christians">
        Iraqi Christians
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Christians" title="Iranian Christians">
        Iranian Christians
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Syria" title="Christianity in Syria">
        Syrian Christians
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_Christians" title="Turkish Christians">
        Turkish Christians
       </a>
       , a label rejected by Assyrians.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Self-designation">
        Self-designation
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Names_of_Syriac_Christians" title="Names of Syriac Christians">
        Names of Syriac Christians
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Below are terms commonly used by Assyrians to self-identify:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         Assyrian
        </b>
        , named after the ancient Assyrian people, is advocated by followers from within all Middle Eastern based
        <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
         East
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/West_Syrian_Rite" title="West Syrian Rite">
         West Syrian Rite
        </a>
        Churches as a catch all term. (see
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
         Syriac Christianity
        </a>
        )
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanDenBerg_121-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-VanDenBerg-121">
          [121]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catholic_Encyclopaedia_128-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Catholic_Encyclopaedia-128">
          [128]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Chaldean
        </b>
        , named after the ancient
        <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
         Chaldean
        </a>
        people, is advocated by some followers of the
        <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
         Chaldean Catholic Church
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Monitor_127-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Al-Monitor-127">
          [127]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Syriac
        </b>
        , named after the
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
         Syriac language
        </a>
        and as a corruption of "Syrian", can be found advocated by followers of the Western Rite
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">
         Syriac Orthodox Church
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">
         Syriac Catholic Church
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-Monitor_127-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Al-Monitor-127">
          [127]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         Aramean
        </b>
        , also known as
        <b>
         West Assyrian
        </b>
        or
        <b>
         Syriac-Aramean
        </b>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minahan2016_129-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Minahan2016-129">
          [129]
         </a>
        </sup>
        named after the ancient
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramean" title="Aramean">
         Aramean
        </a>
        people, is advocated by followers of the Syriac Orthodox Church in Syria
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
         <a href="#cite_note-130">
          [130]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
         <a href="#cite_note-131">
          [131]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and some followers of Syriac Catholic Church in Israel.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
         <a href="#cite_note-132">
          [132]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Some scholars argue that the Aramean identity has become predominant amongst followers of West Syrian churches, and has been partially merged with the Syriac identification.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minahan2016_129-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Minahan2016-129">
          [129]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_vs._Syrian_naming_controversy">
        Assyrian vs. Syrian naming controversy
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As early as the 8th century BC
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luwian" title="Luwian">
        Luwian
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilician" title="Cilician">
        Cilician
       </a>
       subject rulers referred to their Assyrian overlords as
       <i>
        Syrian
       </i>
       , a western
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       corruption of the original term
       <i>
        Assyrian
       </i>
       . This version of the name took hold in the Hellenic lands to the west of the old Assyrian Empire, thus during
       <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
        Greek
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid" title="Seleucid">
        Seleucid
       </a>
       rule from 323 BC the name
       <i>
        Assyria
       </i>
       was altered to
       <i>
        Syria
       </i>
       , and this term was also applied to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramea" title="Aramea">
        Aramea
       </a>
       to the west which had been an Assyrian colony. When the Seleucids lost control of Assyria to the Parthians they retained the corrupted term (Syria), applying it to ancient Aramea, while the Parthians called Assyria "Assuristan," a Parthian form of the original name. It is from this period that the Syrian vs Assyrian controversy arises. Today it is accepted by the majority of scholars that the Medieval, Renaissance and Victorian term
       <i>
        Syriac
       </i>
       when used to describe the indigenous Christians of Mesopotamia and its immediate surrounds in effect means
       <i>
        Aramean
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern terminological problem goes back to colonial times, but it became more acute in 1946, when with the independence of Syria, the adjective
       <i>
        Syrian
       </i>
       referred to an independent state. The controversy isn't restricted to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exonyms" title="Exonyms">
        exonyms
       </a>
       like English "Assyrian" vs. "Aramaean", but also applies to self-designation in Neo-Aramaic, the minority "Aramaean" faction endorses both
       <i>
        Sūryāyē
       </i>
       <span lang="syr">
        ܣܘܪܝܝܐ
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        Ārāmayē
       </i>
       <span lang="syr">
        ܐܪܡܝܐ
       </span>
       , while the majority "Assyrian" faction insists on
       <i>
        Āṯūrāyē
       </i>
       <span lang="syr">
        ܐܬܘܪܝܐ
       </span>
       but also accepts
       <i>
        Sūryāyē
       </i>
       . However, an increasing number of scholars as well as "Syriacs" have begun to use
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramean" title="Aramean">
        Aramean
       </a>
       to refer to this distinct ethnicity (as opposed to ethic
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">
        Assyrians
       </a>
       ) since this is historically, culturally and linguistically a more accurate term.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="449" data-file-width="665" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG/220px-Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG/330px-Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG/440px-Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iraqvillagealqosh.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Alqosh" title="Alqosh">
          Alqosh
         </a>
         , located in the midst of Assyrian contemporary civilization.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The question of ethnic identity and self-designation is sometimes connected to the scholarly debate on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syria_(etymology)" title="Syria (etymology)">
        etymology of "Syria"
       </a>
       . The question has a long history of academic controversy, but majority mainstream opinion currently strongly favours that
       <i>
        Syria
       </i>
       is indeed ultimately derived from the Assyrian term
       <i>
        <b>
         Aššūrāyu
        </b>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Nelson_Frye_Syria_and_Assyria_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Richard_Nelson_Frye_Syria_and_Assyria-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rollinger_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rollinger-135">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Meanwhile, some scholars has disclaimed the theory of Syrian being derived from Assyrian as "simply naive", and detracted its importance to the naming conflict.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
        <a href="#cite_note-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Rudolf Macuch points out that the Eastern Neo-Aramaic press initially used the term "Syrian" (
       <i>
        suryêta
       </i>
       ) and only much later, with the rise of nationalism, switched to "Assyrian" (
       <i>
        atorêta
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to Tsereteli, however, a
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">
        Georgian
       </a>
       equivalent of "Assyrians" appears in ancient Georgian, Armenian and Russian documents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This correlates with the theory of the nations to the East of Mesopotamia knew the group as Assyrians, while to the West, beginning with Greek influence, the group was known as Syrians. Syria being a Greek corruption of Assyria.
      </p>
      <p>
       The debate appears to have been settled by the discovery of the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%87inek%C3%B6y_inscription" title="Çineköy inscription">
        Çineköy inscription
       </a>
       in favour of Syria being derived from Assyria.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Çineköy inscription
       </i>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Hieroglyphic_Luwian" title="Hieroglyphic Luwian">
        Hieroglyphic Luwian
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">
        Phoenician
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Bilingual_inscription" title="Bilingual inscription">
        bilingual
       </a>
       , uncovered from Çineköy,
       <a href="/wiki/Adana_Province" title="Adana Province">
        Adana Province
       </a>
       , Turkey (ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">
        Cilicia
       </a>
       ), dating to the 8th century BC. Originally published by Tekoglu and Lemaire (2000),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it was more recently the subject of a 2006 paper published in the
       <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Near_Eastern_Studies" title="Journal of Near Eastern Studies">
        Journal of Near Eastern Studies
       </a>
       , in which the author, Robert Rollinger, lends support to the age-old debate of the name "Syria" being derived from "Assyria" (see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Syria" title="Etymology of Syria">
        Etymology of Syria
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The object on which the inscription is found is a monument belonging to Urikki,
       <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">
        vassal
       </a>
       king of
       <a href="/wiki/Quw%C3%AA" title="Quwê">
        Hiyawa
       </a>
       (i.e.,
       <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">
        Cilicia
       </a>
       ), dating to the eighth century BC. In this monumental inscription, Urikki made reference to the relationship between his kingdom and his Assyrian overlords. The Luwian inscription reads "Sura/i" whereas the Phoenician translation reads
       <i>
        ’ŠR
       </i>
       or "Ashur" which, according to Rollinger (2006), "settles the problem once and for all".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">
        Culture
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_culture" title="Assyrian culture">
        Assyrian culture
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianclothes23.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Assyrianclothes23.jpg/220px-Assyrianclothes23.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Assyrianclothes23.jpg/330px-Assyrianclothes23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Assyrianclothes23.jpg/440px-Assyrianclothes23.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianclothes23.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Assyrian child dressed in traditional clothes.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="810" data-file-width="1190" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/250px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/375px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg/500px-Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Dialects_EN.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Assyrian dialects.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrian culture is largely influenced by Christianity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
        <a href="#cite_note-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Main festivals occur during religious holidays such as Easter and Christmas. There are also secular holidays such as
       <a href="/wiki/Kha_b-Nisan" title="Kha b-Nisan">
        Kha b-Nisan
       </a>
       (vernal equinox).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       People often greet and bid relatives farewell with a kiss on each cheek and by saying "
       <span lang="syr">
        ܫܠܡܐ ܥܠܝܟ
       </span>
       "
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shlama" title="Shlama">
         Shlama
        </a>
        /Shlomo lokh
       </i>
       , which means: "Peace be upon you" in Neo-Aramaic. Others are greeted with a handshake with the right hand only; according to Middle Eastern customs, the left hand is associated with evil. Similarly, shoes may not be left facing up, one may not have their feet facing anyone directly, whistling at night is thought to waken evil spirits, etc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are many Assyrian customs that are common in other Middle Eastern cultures. A parent will often place an eye pendant on their baby to prevent "an evil eye being cast upon it".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Spitting" title="Spitting">
        Spitting
       </a>
       on anyone or their belongings is seen as a grave insult. Assyrians are
       <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">
        endogamous
       </a>
       , meaning they generally marry within their own ethnic group, although
       <a href="/wiki/Exogamy" title="Exogamy">
        exogamous
       </a>
       marriages are not perceived as a taboo, not unless if the foreigner is of a different religious background, especially a Muslim.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Awoyemi2014_145-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Awoyemi2014-145">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language">
        Language
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic_languages" title="Neo-Aramaic languages">
        Neo-Aramaic languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Neo-Aramaic languages are ultimately descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic_language" title="Old Aramaic language">
        Late Old Eastern Aramaic
       </a>
       , the lingua franca in the later phase of the Neo-Assyrian Empire, displacing the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Semitic" title="East Semitic">
        East Semitic
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
        Assyrian dialect of Akkadian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
        Sumerian
       </a>
       . Aramaic was the language of commerce, trade and communication and became the vernacular language of Assyria in classical antiquity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial_aramaic_148-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-imperial_aramaic-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the 1st century AD, Akkadian was extinct, although its influence on Modern Assyrian is significant and some loaned vocabulary still survives in Assyrian Neo-Aramaic to this day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Akkadian_words_149-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Akkadian_words-149">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaufman_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kaufman-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       To the native speaker, "Syriac" is usually called
       <i>
        Soureth
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Suret
       </i>
       . A wide variety of languages and dialects exist, including
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">
        Assyrian Neo-Aramaic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">
        Chaldean Neo-Aramaic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Turoyo_language" title="Turoyo language">
        Turoyo
       </a>
       . Minority dialects include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senaya" title="Senaya">
        Senaya
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bohtan_Neo-Aramaic" title="Bohtan Neo-Aramaic">
        Bohtan Neo-Aramaic
       </a>
       , which are both
       <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">
        near extinction
       </a>
       . All are classified as Neo-Aramaic languages and are written using
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">
        Syriac script
       </a>
       , a derivative of the ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">
        Aramaic script
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Aramaic" title="Jewish Aramaic">
        Jewish
       </a>
       varieties such as
       <a href="/wiki/Lishanid_Noshan" title="Lishanid Noshan">
        Lishanid Noshan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lish%C3%A1n_Did%C3%A1n" title="Lishán Didán">
        Lishán Didán
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lishana_Deni" title="Lishana Deni">
        Lishana Deni
       </a>
       , written in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_script" title="Hebrew script">
        Hebrew script
       </a>
       , are spoken by Assyrian Jews.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avenery,_Iddo_1988_151-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Avenery,_Iddo_1988-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a considerable amount of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">
        mutual intelligibility
       </a>
       between Assyrian Neo-Aramaic, Chaldean Neo-Aramaic, Senaya, Lishana Deni and Bohtan Neo-Aramaic. Therefore, these "languages" would generally be considered to be dialects of Assyrian Neo-Aramaic rather than separate languages. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Aramaic" title="Jewish Aramaic">
        Jewish Aramaic
       </a>
       languages of Lishan Didan and Lishanid Noshan share a partial intelligibility with these varieties. The mutual intelligibility between the aforementioned languages and Turoyo is, depending on the dialect, limited to partial, and may be asymmetrical.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avenery,_Iddo_1988_151-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Avenery,_Iddo_1988-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Being
       <a href="/wiki/Stateless_nation" title="Stateless nation">
        stateless
       </a>
       , Assyrians are typically multilingual, speaking both their native languages and those of the societies they reside in, such as English,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
        Arabic
       </a>
       , French, German, and Swedish and historically
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">
        Armenian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">
        Azeri
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">
        Kurdish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
        Persian
       </a>
       . Many
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_loanwords_in_Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="List of loanwords in Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">
        loanwords
       </a>
       from the aforementioned languages also exist in the Neo-Aramaic languages. In Turkey (
       <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
        Istanbul
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">
        Tur Abdin
       </a>
       ), northern Syria and northern Iraq,
       <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
        Turkish
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">
        Kurdish
       </a>
       are widely spoken, but only Turkey is reported to be experiencing a population increase of Assyrians in the four countries constituting their historical homeland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Script">
        Script
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">
        Syriac alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrians predominantly use the Syriac script, which is written from right to left. It is one of the
       <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">
        Semitic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">
        abjads
       </a>
       directly descending from the
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">
        Aramaic alphabet
       </a>
       and shares similarities with the
       <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">
        Phoenician
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">
        Hebrew
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">
        Arabic alphabets
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has 22 letters representing consonants, three of which can be
       <a href="/wiki/Mater_lectionis" title="Mater lectionis">
        also
       </a>
       used to indicate vowels. The vowel sounds are supplied either by the reader's memory or by optional
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritic
       </a>
       marks. Syriac is a
       <a href="/wiki/Cursive" title="Cursive">
        cursive
       </a>
       script where some, but not all, letters connect within a word. It was used to write the
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
        Syriac language
       </a>
       from the 1st century AD.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
        <a href="#cite_note-158">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The oldest and classical form of the alphabet is the
       <i>
        ʾEsṭrangēlā
       </i>
       script.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although ʾEsṭrangēlā is no longer used as the main script for writing Syriac, it has received some revival since the
       <a href="/wiki/10th_century" title="10th century">
        10th century
       </a>
       , and it has been added to the
       <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">
        Unicode
       </a>
       Standard in September, 1999. The East Syriac dialect is usually written in the
       <i>
        Maḏnḥāyā
       </i>
       form of the alphabet, which is often translated as "contemporary", reflecting its use in writing modern Neo-Aramaic. The West Syriac dialect is usually written in the
       <i>
        Serṭā
       </i>
       form of the alphabet. Most of the letters are clearly derived from ʾEsṭrangēlā, but are simplified, flowing lines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Furthermore, for practical reasons, Assyrian people would also use the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">
        Latin alphabet
       </a>
       , especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">
        social media
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1024" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg/220px-Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg/330px-Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg/440px-Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christian_Churches.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Historical divisions within Syriac Christian Churches in the Middle East.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
        Syriac Christianity
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrians belong to various
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" title="Christian denominations">
        Christian denominations
       </a>
       such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
        Assyrian Church of the East
       </a>
       , with an estimated 400,000 members,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         [161]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
        Chaldean Catholic Church
       </a>
       , with about 600,000 members,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">
        Syriac Orthodox Church
       </a>
       <i>
        (ʿIdto Suryoyto Triṣaṯ Šuḇḥo)
       </i>
       , which has between 1,000,000 and 4,000,000 members around the world (only some of whom are Assyrians),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
        <a href="#cite_note-163">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">
        Ancient Church of the East
       </a>
       with some 100,000 members. A small minority of Assyrians accepted the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
        Protestant Reformation
       </a>
       in the 20th century, possibly due to British influences, and is now organized in the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Evangelical_Church" title="Assyrian Evangelical Church">
        Assyrian Evangelical Church
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Pentecostal_Church" title="Assyrian Pentecostal Church">
        Assyrian Pentecostal Church
       </a>
       and other Protestant Assyrian groups. While Assyrians are predominantly Christians, a number are irreligious.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many members of the following churches consider themselves Assyrian. Ethnic identities are often deeply intertwined with religion, a legacy of the Ottoman
       <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">
        Millet system
       </a>
       . The group is traditionally characterized as adhering to various churches of Syriac Christianity and speaking Neo-Aramaic languages. It is subdivided into:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        adherents of the
        <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
         Assyrian Church of the East
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">
         Ancient Church of the East
        </a>
        following the
        <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
         East Syrian Rite
        </a>
        also known as
        <i>
         Nestorians
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        adherents of the
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">
         Syriac Orthodox Church
        </a>
        following the
        <a href="/wiki/West_Syrian_Rite" title="West Syrian Rite">
         West Syrian Rite
        </a>
        also known as
        <i>
         Jacobites
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        adherents of the
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Catholic_Church" title="Syriac Catholic Church">
         Syriac Catholic Church
        </a>
        following the West Syrian Rite
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       For obvious reasons the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic" title="Chaldean Catholic">
        Chaldean Catholics
       </a>
       who follow the East Syrian Rite and were originally members of the historical
       <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">
        Church of the East
       </a>
       are not Nestorian in theology, a designation which the Church of the East itself denied.
      </p>
      <p>
       Baptism and First Communion are celebrated extensively, similar to a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brit_Milah" title="Brit Milah">
        Brit Milah
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Bar_and_Bat_Mitzvah" title="Bar and Bat Mitzvah">
        Bar Mitzvah
       </a>
       in Jewish communities. After a death, a gathering is held three days after burial to celebrate the ascension to heaven of the dead person, as of
       <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
        Jesus
       </a>
       ; after seven days another gathering commemorates their death. A close family member wears only black clothes for forty days and nights, or sometimes a year, as a sign of mourning.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
        Music
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian/Syriac_folk_music" title="Assyrian/Syriac folk music">
        Assyrian/Syriac folk music
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Syriac_sacral_music" title="Syriac sacral music">
        Syriac sacral music
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyriankhigga.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1024" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Assyriankhigga.jpg/230px-Assyriankhigga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Assyriankhigga.jpg/345px-Assyriankhigga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Assyriankhigga.jpg/460px-Assyriankhigga.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyriankhigga.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_clothing" title="Traditional clothing">
          Traditional clothing
         </a>
         may be worn in the
         <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_folk_dance" title="Assyrian folk dance">
          Assyrian folk dance
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrian music is a combination of traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">
        folk music
       </a>
       and western contemporary music genres, namely
       <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">
        pop
       </a>
       , but also
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rap_music" title="Rap music">
        rap
       </a>
       and recently,
       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_dance_music" title="Electronic dance music">
        EDM
       </a>
       . Instruments traditionally used by Assyrians include the
       <a href="/wiki/Zurna" title="Zurna">
        zurna
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Davul" title="Davul">
        davula
       </a>
       , but has expanded to include guitars, pianos, violins, synthesizers (keyboards and
       <a href="/wiki/Electronic_drum" title="Electronic drum">
        electronic drums
       </a>
       ), and other instruments.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some well known Assyrian singers in modern times are
       <a href="/wiki/Ashur_Bet_Sargis" title="Ashur Bet Sargis">
        Ashur Bet Sargis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sargon_Gabriel" title="Sargon Gabriel">
        Sargon Gabriel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Evin_Agassi" title="Evin Agassi">
        Evin Agassi
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Janan_Sawa" title="Janan Sawa">
        Janan Sawa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Juliana_Jendo" title="Juliana Jendo">
        Juliana Jendo
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Linda_George_(Assyrian_singer)" title="Linda George (Assyrian singer)">
        Linda George
       </a>
       . Assyrian artists that traditionally sing in other languages include
       <a href="/wiki/Melechesh" title="Melechesh">
        Melechesh
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Timz" title="Timz">
        Timz
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Aril_Brikha" title="Aril Brikha">
        Aril Brikha
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The first International
       <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_Music_Festival" title="Aramaic Music Festival">
        Aramaic Music Festival
       </a>
       was held in Lebanon in August 2008 for Assyrian people internationally.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dance">
        Dance
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_folk_dance" title="Assyrian folk dance">
        Assyrian folk dance
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrians have numerous traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Dance" title="Dance">
        dances
       </a>
       which are performed mostly for special occasions such as weddings. Assyrian dance is a blend of both ancient indigenous and general near eastern elements. Assyrian folk dances are mainly made up of
       <a href="/wiki/Circle_dance" title="Circle dance">
        circle dances
       </a>
       that are performed in a line, which may be straight, curved, or both. Most of the dances allow unlimited number of participants, with the exception of the
       <i>
        Sabre Dance
       </i>
       , which require three at most. Assyrian dances would vary from weak to strong, depending on the mood and
       <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">
        tempo
       </a>
       of a song.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">
        Festivals
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Assyrian festivals tend to be closely associated with their Christian faith, of which
       <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">
        Easter
       </a>
       is the most prominent of the celebrations. Members of the Assyrian Church of the East, Chaldean Catholic Church and Syriac Catholic Church follow the
       <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">
        Gregorian calendar
       </a>
       and as a result celebrate Easter on a Sunday between March 22 and April 25 inclusively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
        <a href="#cite_note-164">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, members of the Syriac Orthodox Church and Ancient Church of the East celebrate Easter on a Sunday between April 4 and May 8 inclusively on the Gregorian calendar (March 22 and April 25 on the
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">
        Julian calendar
       </a>
       ). During
       <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">
        Lent
       </a>
       , Assyrians are encouraged to fast for 50 days from meat and any other foods which are animal based.
      </p>
      <p>
       Assyrians celebrate a number of festivals unique to their culture and traditions as well as religious ones:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Kha_b-Nisan" title="Kha b-Nisan">
         Kha b-Nisan
        </a>
        <i>
         <span class="script-syrn" dir="rtl" style="font-family:'East Syriac Adiabene', 'East Syriac Ctesiphon', 'Noto Sans Syriac Eastern', FreeSans, Code2000; font-size:125%;">
          ܚܕ ܒܢܝܣܢ
         </span>
         ‬
        </i>
        , the Assyrian New Year, traditionally on April 1, though usually celebrated on January 1. Assyrians usually wear traditional costumes and hold social events including parades and parties, dancing, and listening to poets telling the story of creation.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
         <a href="#cite_note-165">
          [165]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauma_d-Ba%27utha" title="Sauma d-Ba'utha">
         Sauma d-Ba'utha
        </a>
        <i>
         <span class="script-syrn" dir="rtl" style="font-family:'East Syriac Adiabene', 'East Syriac Ctesiphon', 'Noto Sans Syriac Eastern', FreeSans, Code2000; font-size:125%;">
          ܒܥܘܬܐ ܕܢܝܢܘܝܐ
         </span>
         ‬
        </i>
        , the Nineveh fast, is a three-day period of fasting and prayer.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SycOrth_166-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-SycOrth-166">
          [166]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Somikka, All Saints Day, is celebrated to motivate children to fast during Lent through use of frightening costumes
       </li>
       <li>
        Kalu d'Sulaqa, feast of the Bride of the Ascension, celebrates Assyrian resistance to the invasion of Assyria by Tamerlane
       </li>
       <li>
        Nusardyl, commemorating the baptism of the Assyrians of Urmia by St. Thomas.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cultureofiran.com_167-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cultureofiran.com-167">
          [167]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Sharra d'Mart Maryam, usually on August 15, a festival and feast celebrating St. Mary with games, food, and celebration.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cultureofiran.com_167-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-cultureofiran.com-167">
          [167]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Other Sharras (special festivals) include: Sharra d'Mart Shmuni, Sharra d'Mar Shimon Bar-Sabbaye, Sharra d'Mar Mari, and Shara d'Mar Zaia, Mar Bishu, Mar Sawa, Mar Sliwa, and Mar Odisho
       </li>
       <li>
        Yoma d'Sah'deh (Day of Martyrs), commemorating the thousands massacred in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simele_Massacre" title="Simele Massacre">
         Simele Massacre
        </a>
        and the hundreds of thousands massacred in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" title="Assyrian Genocide">
         Assyrian Genocide
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Assyrians also practice unique marriage ceremonies. The rituals performed during weddings are derived from many different elements from the past 3,000 years. An Assyrian wedding traditionally lasted a week. Today, weddings in the Assyrian homeland usually last 2–3 days; in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_diaspora" title="Assyrian diaspora">
        Assyrian diaspora
       </a>
       they last 1–2 days.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_clothing">
        Traditional clothing
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_clothing" title="Assyrian clothing">
        Assyrian clothing
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrian clothing varies from village to village. Clothing is usually blue, red, green, yellow, and purple; these colors are also used as embroidery on a white piece of clothing. Decoration is lavish in Assyrian costumes, and sometimes involves jewellery. The conical hats of traditional Assyrian dress have changed little over millennia from those worn in ancient Mesopotamia, and until the 19th and early 20th centuries the ancient Mesopotamian tradition of braiding or platting of hair, beards and moustaches was still commonplace.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">
        Cuisine
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_cuisine" title="Assyrian cuisine">
        Assyrian cuisine
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Assyrian cuisine is similar to other Middle Eastern cuisines. It is rich in
       <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">
        grain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">
        meat
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">
        potato
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">
        cheese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">
        bread
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">
        tomato
       </a>
       . Typically,
       <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">
        rice
       </a>
       is served with every meal, with a stew poured over it.
       <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">
        Tea
       </a>
