       is a popular drink, and there are several dishes of desserts, snacks, and beverages.
       <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">
        Alcoholic
       </a>
       drinks such as
       <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">
        wine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wheat_beer" title="Wheat beer">
        wheat beer
       </a>
       are organically produced and drank.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">
        Genetics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_Near_East" title="Genetic history of the Near East">
        Genetic history of the Near East
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Late 20th century DNA analysis conducted by
       <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Luca_Cavalli-Sforza" title="Luigi Luca Cavalli-Sforza">
        Cavalli-Sforza
       </a>
       , Paolo Menozzi and Alberto Piazza, "shows that Assyrians have a distinct genetic profile that distinguishes their population from any other population."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assyrianfoundation.org_168-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-assyrianfoundation.org-168">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Genetic analysis of the Assyrians of Persia demonstrated that they were "closed" with little "intermixture" with the Muslim Persian population and that an individual Assyrian's genetic makeup is relatively close to that of the Assyrian population as a whole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         [169]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
        <a href="#cite_note-170">
         [170]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "The genetic data are compatible with historical data that religion played a major role in maintaining the Assyrian population's separate identity during the Christian era".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assyrianfoundation.org_168-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-assyrianfoundation.org-168">
         [168]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a 2006 study of the Y chromosome DNA of six regional Armenian populations, including, for comparison, Assyrians and Syrians, researchers found that, "the Semitic populations (Assyrians and Syrians) are very distinct from each other according to both [comparative] axes. This difference supported also by other methods of comparison points out the weak genetic affinity between the two populations with different historical destinies."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iran_and_the_Caucasus_171-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Iran_and_the_Caucasus-171">
         [171]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 2008 study on the genetics of "old ethnic groups in Mesopotamia," including 340 subjects from seven ethnic communities ("Assyrian, Jewish, Zoroastrian, Armenian, Turkmen, the Arab peoples in Iran, Iraq, and Kuwait") found that Assyrians were homogeneous with respect to all other ethnic groups sampled in the study, regardless of religious affiliation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pubmed.gov_172-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pubmed.gov-172">
         [172]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a 2011 study focusing on the genetics of Marsh Arabs of Iraq, researchers identified Y chromosome haplotypes shared by Marsh Arabs, Iraqis, and Assyrians, "supporting a common local background."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology_173-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology-173">
         [173]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In a 2017 study focusing on the genetics of Northern Iraqi populations, it was found that Iraqi Assyrians and Iraqi Yazidis clustered together, but away from the other Northern Iraqi populations analyzed in the study, and largely in between the West Asian and Southeastern European populations. According to the study, "contemporary Assyrians and Yazidis from Northern Iraq may in fact have a stronger continuity with the original genetic stock of the Mesopotamian people, which possibly provided the basis for the ethnogenesis of various subsequent Near Eastern populations".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         [174]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

        <li id="cite_note-122">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-122">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.friesian.com/notes/note-n.htm" rel="nofollow">
            "Note on the Modern Assyrians, &amp; Other Nationalistic Issues"
           </a>

           <i>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=friesian.com&amp;rft.atitle=Note+on+the+Modern+Assyrians%2C+%26+Other+Nationalistic+Issues&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.friesian.com%2Fnotes%2Fnote-n.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyrian+people">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-Cross_2005-123">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Cross_2005_123-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Cross, Frank Leslie (2005).
           <i>
            The Oxford Dictionary of the Christian Church
           </i>
           . Oxford University Press. p. 119.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780192802903" title="Special:BookSources/9780192802903">
            9780192802903
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            In the 19th cent. A. H. Layard, the excavator of Nineveh, first suggested that the local *Syriac Christian communities in the region were descended from the ancient Assyrians, and the idea was later popularized by W. A. Wigram, a member of the Abp. Of Canterbury’s Mission to the Church of the East (1895-1915).
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Dictionary+of+the+Christian+Church&amp;rft.pages=119&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=9780192802903&amp;rft.aulast=Cross&amp;rft.aufirst=Frank+Leslie&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyrian+people">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-124">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-124">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           Jonathan Eric Lewis.
           <a class="external text" href="http://www.meforum.org/article/558" rel="nofollow">
            "Iraqi Assyrians: Barometer of Pluralism"
           </a>
           .
           <i>
            Middle East Forum
           </i>
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             18 February
            </span>
            2015
           </span>

        <li id="cite_note-Iran_and_the_Caucasus-171">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Iran_and_the_Caucasus_171-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.rau.am/downloads/publ.kafedr/episkoposyan_medbiolog/Yepiskoposian_I&amp;C_06.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Yepiskoposian et al., Iran and the Caucasus, Volume 10, Number 2, 2006, pp. 191-208(18), "Genetic Testing of Language Replacement Hypothesis in Southwest Asia"
          </a>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-pubmed.gov-172">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-pubmed.gov_172-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation journal">
           Banoei, M. M.; Chaleshtori, M. H.; Sanati, M. H.; Shariati, P; Houshmand, M; Majidizadeh, T; Soltani, N. J.; Golalipour, M (Feb 2008). "Variation of DAT1 VNTR alleles and genotypes among old ethnic groups in Mesopotamia to the Oxus region".
           <i>
            Hum Biol
           </i>
           .
           <b>
            80
           </b>
           (1): 73–81.
           <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
            doi
           </a>
           :
           <a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.3378%2F1534-6617%282008%2980%5B73%3AVODVAA%5D2.0.CO%3B2" rel="nofollow">
            10.3378/1534-6617(2008)80[73:VODVAA]2.0.CO;2
           </a>
           .
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">
            PMID
           </a>
           <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18505046" rel="nofollow">
            18505046
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            The relationship probability was lowest between Assyrians and other communities.
            <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">
             Endogamy
            </a>
            was found to be high for this population through determination of the heterogeneity coefficient (+0,6867), Our study supports earlier findings indicating the relatively closed nature of the Assyrian community as a whole, which as a result of their religious and cultural traditions, have had little intermixture with other populations.
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Hum+Biol&amp;rft.atitle=Variation+of+DAT1+VNTR+alleles+and+genotypes+among+old+ethnic+groups+in+Mesopotamia+to+the+Oxus+region&amp;rft.volume=80&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=73-81&amp;rft.date=2008-02&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3378%2F1534-6617%282008%2980%5B73%3AVODVAA%5D2.0.CO%3B2&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F18505046&amp;rft.aulast=Banoei&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+M.&amp;rft.au=Chaleshtori%2C+M.+H.&amp;rft.au=Sanati%2C+M.+H.&amp;rft.au=Shariati%2C+P&amp;rft.au=Houshmand%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Majidizadeh%2C+T&amp;rft.au=Soltani%2C+N.+J.&amp;rft.au=Golalipour%2C+M&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyrian+people">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology-173">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology_173-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.biomedcentral.com/content/pdf/1471-2148-11-288.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           Al-Zahery et al., BMC Evolutionary Biology 2011, 11:288, "In search of the genetic footprints of Sumerians: a survey of Y-chromosome and mtDNA variation in the Marsh Arabs of Iraq"
          </a>
          "In the less frequent J1-M267* clade, only marginally affected by events of expansion, Marsh Arabs shared haplotypes with other Iraqi and Assyrian samples, supporting a common local background."
         </span>
